5.14      These issues are well known amongstforest policy-makersbut were
recently revisited on the State and the Bank by the ReviewTeam. Devastation to
intact mixed species of tropical forests can be profound. Bankforest experts
should be aware of this because it has been demonstrated in the literature see
 Bowles, et al, "Logging and Tropical Conservation''Sciencevolume 280, 19June
 1998,p1899,see Appendix 15. Closely associatedwith the degradation of the
forest, the degradation of the culture we have referredto above. As Schmid
 notes at Appendix 14, the immediate impacts of selective logging may not be
 visible. High grading sees the removal of select trees only and the lower value
 softwoodsare left in place. The appearance is that an intact forest is still there.
 The under-growthand vines quickly re-establishthemselves. In reality a profound
 change has taken place, althoughthere may be little or no perception of the
 change. The Claimantsfears of this global phenomenonmay have a basis in
 Papua New Guinea. Here,the fears are well established,and have been
 documented.


 5.15     Rotationcycles. The PNG ForestAuthority bases all its forest
 managementon a 35-year forestry cycle or rotation period. There is no scientific
 evidence to support the propositionthat mixed speciestropical rainforest will
 regeneratewithin a period of 35 years after they have been logged. The
 evidence is to the contrary. High value tropical hardwood species invariably take
 longer than 35 years to regenerate.The ReviewTeam has recommendedthat
 the Forest Research Institute be asked to advise the State on the issue of
 rotation period. But that is not enough. There needsto be a breathing space long

                                      P A G E 2 0

enough for all stakeholders to view and discuss the data. That would take time.
Certainly no new concessions should be allocated untilthe rotation period is
scientifically estabiished, to the satisfactionof stakeholders.This issue is linked
to Bank policy in OP 4.36. The definition of "sustainablemanagement" inthe
Bank's Policy is:

             "(e) Sustainablemanagementof natural forests means controlled
                utilization of the resource to produce woodand non wood benefits
                into perpetuity, with the basic objectives of long-term maintenance
                of forest cover and appropriate reservation of areas for biodiversity
                protection and other ecological purposes."


  If the resource is to be used in perpetuity,then the rotation period must reflect
  the life cycle of the slow-growing species. It must be scientifically based,and
  not an arbitrary figure such as 35 or 40 years.


5.16      "Sustainable"managementof naturalforests. The policy mandatein
 OP 4.36 of sustainable management of naturalforests, is problematic in Papua
 New Guinea's mixed-speciesforests. The evidence points to industrial scale
 sustainable forestry not being either economicallyor ecologically feasible. One
 reasonfor industrial-scaleforestry not beingfeasible, is that the cost of putting
 back the forest into its original form is very high.True sustainable forest
 managementin mixed species tropical forests has a very high cost of
 managementto make it work. This is so for example, with the Mil Madeiras
 experiment inAmazonas, and other Brazilian examplesthat are Forest
 Stewardship Council certified. The claimants assertthat there are no examples
 in the world where industrial logging in a mixed-speciestropical forest has been
 shown to be ecologically or commerciallysustainable. Papua New Guinea
 forests and their owners are being usedfor experimental purposes, at great risk
 and without scientific or economicjustification.


 5.17     Selective logging. Another reasonwhy the sustainable managementof
 naturalforests is problematic in Papua New Guinea, is that selective logging, on
 an industrial scale, tends to kill-off the high value slow-growing hardwoods.The
 Bank policy in OP 4.36 :


           "TheBank's lending operations in the forest sector are conditional on
          government commitment to undertakesustainable managementand
          conservation-orientedforestry':


  is not followed in that the selective logging destroys between 53 to 79 % of the
 high value trees. Ittherefore cannot be called "conservation-orientatedForestry",
 see paras 5.23-24 supra.


 5.18     The standard logging method of operation used by the PNG Forest
 Authority is to employ a technique known as "selective logging". This technique
 was used on the Claimants land without their consent, along the alignment of the
 Kiungakiambak road, and will be used along the alignment of the Baramundi
 Highway.


 5.19      The term "selective logging" is a eEphemismfor "high-grading".With
                                      P A G E 2 1

 Iilyri-graaing"the logger removesthe high-valuedtropical hardwoodspecies,
which are generally slower growing. The effect of this is to give living space to
the faster growing, but commerciallyless valuable, softwood species. The
ecological balance of the forest is disrilpted and a systematic degradationtakes
place into secondary growth.



5.20      Logging companies, with the PNG ForestAuthority, turning a blind eye or
in connivance,then carry out a second cut, even though a second cut would not
be in accordancewith the law. The implementationof the Logging Code of
Conduct is poor. Forest managementis poor. There has never been a
prosecutionof a logging companyfor violation of the Logging Code of Conduct. A
second cut would not conform to environmentalstandards. Environmental Impact
Statements are routinely signed-off by Ministersfor Conservationand
Environmentwithout regard to standards. The Office of Environmentand
Conservation has closed its sectionfor monitoringforests. There has never been
a prosecution 07 a logging companyfor infringementof environmental
regulations. Following the second-cut,the forest is so degraded,the logging
company then recommendsto the State, generally through the Departmentof
Primary !ndustry,the clearance of all trees, and the planting of oil palm.The
 logging company, the Departmentof Primary Industry,the PNG ForestAuthority,
the Office of Conservationand Environment,the Departmentof Lands and the
 Provincial Governmentthen work together to set up an "oil palm scheme".
Agricultural clearances are not "selective logging. They are clear-fell operations.
 They are therefore profitable for loggers,who can fell all the trees.


 5.21     Collingwood Bay Case: 38000 ha "stolen".A specific example of this
 attempt at a clear-fell operation, occurred in 1999, at Collingwood Bay. There,
 State entitiesworking together, unlawfullyalienated 38,000 ha of customary land
 and placed it under indefeasibletitle. The customary landowners obtained interim
 injunctions stopping actual logging, but the matter is still before the court. And the
 title over the land is still in existence,although disputed.


 5.22      In 1999,the State permittedthe alleged fraudulent acquisition of
 38,000ha of forestsfor an illegal oil palm project at Collingwood Bay, in the Oro
 Province of Papua New Guinea. This illegal acquisition undermined attempts by
 local communities to protect their forests and pursue alternative sustainable
 development paths. The Maisin peoplewere able to establish amongst
 themselves local control over their lands and their forests. Their leadershipwas a
 combination of traditional leaders, and educated people who had either returned
 from work inthe modernsector, or kept close links with home, while still at work
  in government or commerce. The leadership,working though a complex system
  of local consultationarrived at a practiceof forest conservation. No cutting the
 forest, not even with portable sawmills. As an alternative, they developed links
 with NGOs to markettraditional bark-cloth-known as tapa-cloth, When the
  landowners learntabout the leases, they successfully sued for interim orders
  preventing any trespass.The action is still before the courts. Landowners
  sought and obtained interim injunctions to stop logging in 1999. The loggers
  through their lawyers brought a series of interlocutory applications, culminating in

                                        P A G E 2 2

Forest Authority, although defendantsin this action, have done nothing to defend
the rights of the landowners in that case. The landowners' lawyersgave a full
brief on the case to Bank officials.


5.23     "70%of stems left mortallywounded" Mr. T R Vigus, a respected
forester and conservationist in Papua New Guinea, undertook research inthe
1980s and 199O's,which empirically shows the high loss of commercialtrees
after selective logging has beenthrough a forest. The data was obtainedfrom
plots within commercial timber concessionsin Papua New Guinea, and show
between a 53% to 79% loss of commercialtrees after selective logging. See
Appendix 16.


5.24     Vigus work is cited and supportedby other scientists. C. K Schmid says:

                  "In selective logging those big mature trees whichare anyway
                   not used by local people are removed by the loggers and paid
                    for. Thisprocess is often unprofessionallycarried out by poorly
                    skilled personnel operating heavy equipment.

                   "In research plots in WestNew Britain it was shown that more
                    than 70% of the stems wereleft mortally wounded (Cameron
                    and Vigus 1993),not to mention collaterai damage to other
                    forest organisms and trees. Withinthe WawoiGuavi Timber
                    Concession in the SouthernHighlands, Brunios concluded that
                    an average of 119trees were damages in every 10,000square
                    metres of selectively logged forest (Brunios 1999b,9), or, that
                    for every selectively felled tree, 45 others were mortally
                    wounded." (Brunios1999b,31).

 million= US$ 36 million.

 There is a symmetrybetweenthis net lossto rheClaimantsof US$36 million,
 andthe SecondTrancheoutstandingto the State of US$35 million.

 5.36     Other lossesinclude:

           damageto the land, herethe Claimantsare entitledto be compensated
           to the value of the cost of repairsto the landto re-instatethe land in the
           conditionitwas beforelogging

           damageto the environmentincludingloss of species; the quantumof
                                     P A G E 2 6

       these damages is yet to be ascertained. The principle is that where
        damages are general, and not special or readilyquantifiable by market
        values etc, then assessors have to do the best they can with the
        materialsavailable. It will depend upona scientific assessment of the
        impact of logging on the environmentand species. Such a qualification
        will be difficult because no base-linewas done beforethe illegal logging
        commence. Neverthelessthese damages are recognisedat law, and in
        principle,the calculation would proceedon the basis of such factual and
        scientific material as was availableat the time of assessment.They
        would include damagefor the proven absence of riverinecreatures,
        birds, mammals, and flora, irrespective of whether or not the species
        had an economic use. In addition, where the species had an economic
        or social use, then the damagewould need to be assessed on the loss
        of the value of that use.

    =    damagesfor adverse social impacts may includethe value of extrawork
         needed to carry water, find food, collect building materials;a developer
         in a remotearea should exercise a duty of care in respect of the impact
         of introduc`ed exotic diseases, particularlyvenereal diseases and AIDS.
         Both are known to follow "highways" into remote areas, particularlyif a
         developer can be shown to have neglectedto providefor employee
         codes of conduct, the banningof drugs and alcohol, and non-
         fraternisationpolicies, as can be seen in the practices of other socially
         responsible industries.

         damages for trespass per se. The Claimantsare entitled to a sum
         irrespectiveof compensationfor puttingthem, and the land, backto
         where it/ they were before logging.This sum is for the trespass, in the
         natureof punitive damage.

The Claimants'rightsand interestsare damaged because Bank officials have
not ensuredthe loan conditions promotedgood governance in their forests, and
 have been implemented by Bank officials in such a way as to permit the State
to cause damage and harmto our clients. The actions and omissions of the
 officials in violation of Bank policy have caused, and are likely to continueto
 cause,the claimants to suffer harm.

The Claimantsare under pressurebecausethey have beentold that if the Bank
pays out the secondtranche beforethe Claimants'concernsare addressed,that
they may losetheir opportunityto approach the InspectionPanel, because Bank
 lawyerswill arguethat "the projectis complete".

 There is an alternativeview that the loan is only complete,when it is repaid,
 twentyyears hence.

 To holdthe view that a loan is completeafter loanfunds are disbursed is
 artificial, goes against commercialpracticeand common sense.At law and in
 common commercialpractice,loansare completewhen they are repaid.

 Whatever the legaleffect of theseviews, the Claimants are hard-pressedto
 invokethe assistanceof the Panelbecause there is a view that once the second
 tranche is paid out they have no recourseto the Panel.

 Customarylandownersand nongovernment organizationshave continuallyand
 repeatedcalled on Bank officialsto ensure a moratoriumon newforest
 concessionsis imposed, as partof the conditionsof any loan, while a review of
 all concessionstakes place. This is necessaryto ensure that forests are properly
 managed, benefitssharedfairly and irreparabledamage does nottake placeto
 the Claimants'forests.

Recent DevelopmentsRegardingThese Issues

The State hasannouncedthat the moratoriumwill be lifted as of 30thNovember
2001.

There is no NationalForestPlan,'no properforest inventories,and no informed
consentover the alienationof timber rightsfrom traditionallandownersand very
little abilityto manageforests or the environment. All these mattershave been
confirmed by the ReviewTeam. Inthese circumstances,it is irresponsibleto
proceedto allocatemoreforests to the loggers.

Bank officialssay they have reachedagreementwith the Statethat the
recommendationsof the ReviewTeamwill be enforcedthroughthe Forestand
Conservation Project.Similarly, Bank officials say that no new projectwill go
`orward without an independentreview. We have not seen the text of that
agreement,and are not preparedto acceptthose assurances,for two reasons.
First,the State has a bad recordof honouringits agreementsinforestry andthe
Bankhas a poor record in enforcingthem. That is what this claim is really about.
Secondly,the State is proceedingto allocateconcessionswithout independent
review.

Receiptof correspondencefromthe Bank has informedus that the liftingof the
Moratoriumwill go aheadand for us to raise our concernswith throughthe
Governmentand or the PapuaNew GuineaForestAuthority.The Bank did not
haveany standingto intervenein a matterthat came under PapuaNew Guinea's
jurisdiction.See Appendix 33.

 In light of the reasonablelack of trust explainedabove,vague assurancesare
 unsatisfactory.The claimantscall on the World Bank to withholddisbursementof
the trancheof the SALand requirethe Governmentto maintainthe moratorium
 until:

       1. The KiungaAiambak road is cancelledand any companythat was found
           to be in violationof the law has its ForestIndustry Participantstatus
           revokedpursuantto the ForestryAct.

       2. A full review of existing loggingconcessionsis completedand it's
            recommendationsimplemented.

       3. The findings andthe recommendationsof the World Bank Review
           Team's Reporton pendingloggingconcessionsmust befully
            implementedand there must be ongoing independentreviewof any
            Timber Permit applications.

 9.         In addition we havetaken the followingsteps to

                                      P A G E 3 2

In 1998,an NGOcalledthe Individualand CommunityRightsAdvocacy Forum
madea formal complaintto the PNGOmbudsmanCommission. About the
unlawful natureof loggingon the KiungaAiambak Road.Therewas evidence of
an inducementbeingpaidfor an exemptionto the export logtax. A notice
Tppeared in the NationalGazetteof the logtax\ exemption. The Ombudsman
;ommission began itsformal investigation.The ministerresignedhis ministry
and seat in Parliamentciting his needto spend moretimewith his family. The
OmbudsmanCommissiontook the view that the matter was better handledby
the police,and stopped its investigation.The policenever pursuedthe case.

In2000, a law suit instituted by land owners otherthanthe Claimants, led by
PaulSingi, engaged privatelawyers,and sought orders from the NationalCourt
to stop loggingon the KiungaAiambak road.The ForestAuthority and the State
were amongstthe defendants in that action. The loggingcompaniessuccessfully
obtainedan orderto strike out ihe landowners'action,on the basis of procedural
error. Costswere awardedagainst the landownerplaintiffsin an amount of K19,
462.75. SeeAppendix 34. The Claimantshave been informedby the lawyersfor
Mr. Singi,that the lawyers representingthe loggingcompanieswere assisted
out-of-courtby a very senior governmentlawyer.

On the 4th of September 2001 a policeman,who is-anativeto the area,filed a
formal complaintwith the OmbudsmanCommissionand calledfor an
investigationintoallegationsof unlawful and improper policeactivitiesassociated
with the loggingproject.See Appendix 35.

 In 2001, the Claimants'and their lawyers have receivedanonymousthreats, and
are currentlyseekingways of bringtheir complaintsto courtwithout exposing
themselvesto'violence. Attempts are beingmadeto engage a largefirm of
 lawyersto take on the case.

From:      Brian Brunton<greenpeace@png.pactok.net>
To:        James Douglas <jdougIas@worldbank.org>
Date:      Thursday, 4 November 199916:37
Subject: PNGforests and the World Bank


Jim,

this is my version of a summary of our conversationon Tuesday2nd of
November 1999inthe World Bankoffice, Islander,PortMoresby.

1. The Bankwas concernedabout the deteriorationinforest managementin
PNG, andwould requestthe GoPNG, as part of conditionalityon both SAP and
forest loansto introduce a moratoriumon all newforest projectswhether in
the form of FMAsor agricultural clearancesor road-lineclearances

2. the Bankwould supportlproposea reviewof all existingprojects
during the duration of the moratorium.

3. the Bankwas very clearthat thereshould be no relaxingof the export
tog tax.

4. Greenpeace askedthe Bankto conveyto the PrimeMiinisterthe
concernsover the allocationof the Kamula Dosoconcessionto RH in February

DetailedSubmissionson Imprudenceand Irresponsibility


Inour submission, Bankersshouldnot lendmoneyto personswho levelof competency is so
pcurasto raisereasonableinferencesovertheirhonesty.Bankersprudencerequiresa
reasonablelevelof inquiryandcommonsense.

Bankreportsanddocumentsshowa historyof corruptionandillegalityinforest management
andconmation in PapuaNewGuinea.Thistrendwas consistentwithexperienceelsewhere
andshould havebeenwell-knownas danger-signs to officials:

      "illegalforestryactivities serveto directlyunderminethe bank's objectives of
     povertyreductionand sustainabledevelopment indeedthey havehad"a range
     of negztlveeconomic,ecologicaland social impactsandact as a direct
     impedimentto achievingsustainableforest managemenr'

     (Callister,DJCompfionand IllegalActivitiesintheFomstrySecfor:CUlTenf
     undersfandings,andimpficafionsfor WorldBank ForestryPolicy,World BankGroup
     ForestPolicyImplementationReviewandStrategy DevelopmentAnalyticalStudies,
     WashingtonDC, May 1999p6)


Thecorruptionin Papua NewGuineawas "grand corruption"andthe Bankwas awarethat in
the industry "facilitationfees" were routineinAsia (id 23)-andthatAsian companies
dominatedthe PNG markets (seefor examplefn 16GreenpeaceBuying Destruction),and
thatthe effectofforest industrycorruption in PapuaNewGuineawasto destabilize
govemance. Corruptionin PapuaNewGuinea's forestserodedpublictrust inthegovernment
jib 29)

The Prime Minister, Sir MekereMorautahasstatedopenlythat corruption is bothsystematic
andsystemic.

Inour submission, Bankofficialsmisjudgedthe amount andquality of corruptionassociated
with PapuaNewGuinea's rainforests. Loggingcompaniestraditionally fund politicians,
politicalpartiesandelections.The electioncycledrivescandidatesto enter indebtas they
canvassandcampaign. The needtofund electionexpenses, andthe debt makesthem
vulnerable to loggingcompanies. These pressures are curentlystrong because2002 is an
electionyear inPapuaNewGuinea.

In 1999theWorld BankleePapuaNewGuineabecausethe levelsof corruption, andthe
presenceof anex-WorldBankstaff, employedbythe PapuaNewGuineangovemmentas a
consultant.All inall, at thatpointintime, illegality,andpoorgovernancewere so high
generally that itwas seento be imprudentto continue bankingbusiness see Fileret a1idpp
70-73. InMid1999,the govemment of the HonW Skate, lost officeonthefloorof
Parliament. Theex-Bankstafferkonsultant,was sacked(andsubsequentlyjailed); andthe
Bankreturnedto PapuaNewGuineatodo business.

Bankofficialspersuadedthemselvesthat the new prime ministershouldbe supported.
Howeverthey misjudged, amongst other things, the extent towhich the logging industrywas
ableto infiuencedecision-makingwithinthe new PrimeMinister'scoalition.

InFebruary 1999,the Boardof the PNGForestAuthority, withoutgoingto publictenders,
granted KamulaDosoFMA (8OO,OCBhaj,as anextensionof the adjacent340,000ha
concession. The Boardwas forcedtocancelthat extension,andan Ombudsman Commission
investigationis still proceeding. The logging company threatenedto suethe PNGForest

Authority.The unlawfulallocationof Kamula Dosowas one of the main reasonsfor Bank
Hicials acceptingthe needfor a moratorium:see Fileret a1idp82 .

DetailedSubmissionson Breachesof QP4.36

Bankdecisionsin PapuaNew Guineaforest mattersare regulatedbythe Bank's 1993forest
policy knownas Operational Policy4.36 (OP 4.36). The policy statesthat:

     "Bankinvolvementintheforestry sector aimsto reducedeforestation,enhance
    the environmentalcontributionofforestedareas, promoteafforestation, reduce
    poverty, andencourageeconomicdevelopment".

Thisbindingpolicy document prohibitsthe Bankfromfinancingcommerciallogging
operationsor the purchaseof loggingequipmentfor useintropicalmoistprimaryforest such
as in PNG. Where a country hasmadea commitmentto undertake"conservation-orientated"
forestry, the Bankmayfinanceimprovementsinplanning, monitoringandfieldcontrol of
loggingoperations.

The implicationsof this policyfor BanklendinginPNGarethat the only lendingthat the Bank
should permit isfor improvingthe planning,monitoringandfieldcontrolof loggingoperations,
andthis is only permissibleif the PNGgovemmenthasmadea credible commitmentto
conservationorientatedforestry.

InPNGthe govemmenthasmadenocrediblecommitmenttoconservationorientated
forestry, indeedforestmanagementisso poorasto causeactualharmtotheforestsandto
thequalityof lifeof localpeople. This harmresultsina degradationof the ancient rainforest,
tumingthem intopoorsecondaryforest.

1. The PNGForestAuthority basesall itsforest managementon a 35 yearbrestry cycleor
    rotationperiod. There issimplynoevidenceto supportthe propositionthatmixedspecies
    tropical rainforestwill regeneratewithina periodof 35 years after they havebeen logged.
    The evidenceisto thecontrary. Tropical hardwoodstake longerthan40 yearsto grow to
    maturity.The ReviewTeam recommendedthat this issuebestudied bythe Forest
    ResearchInstitute. Thisstudy should beconductedinconsultationwithlandownersand
    NGOsandthe resultsimplemented inpolicyandpracticebeforeany newconcessions
    areallowed

2. Bankofficials, andMr. Douglasinparticular,wereconcemedtoensure continuity in
    industriallogging inPapuaNewGuinea. These officialshistoricallywere committedto a
    dogma of logginginmixedspeciestropical forests, eventhoughthe preponderanceof the
    weight of the literature,science, andcommercial practice,tendedto showthat industrial
    applications inthesecircumstancesarenot sustainable. The positionof Mr. Douglasand
    BankofficialscontradictedtheBank's policy OP4.36 asthere is nocredible evidenceto
    suggest that industrialloggingin PNGcontributestothe policy aims of reducing
    deforestation, enhancingthe environmentalcontributionof forestedareas, promoting
    afforestation, reducingpoverty andencouragingeconomicdevelopment.

3. The fact that the PNG govemment has no credible commitment to conservation
    orientated forestry is indicated by the Review Team findings on fragile forests,
    conservationset-asides andreliableresourcedescriptions. The ReviewTeam found that
    currently there is no policydecision preventing !egging infragileforest areas, a provision
    for conservation set-asides is not beingimplementedandthere is no amrate or reliable
    resourcedata beingused.

4. The lack of a crediblecommitmentto conservationorientatedforestry is also indicatedin
    the governments failure to planfor forestry operations ai either a national or provincial
    level. Despitethe fact that the Foresfry Act section54 mandates that there be a National

ForestPlan, beforeanyconcessioncan beallocated, there is no current NationalForest
Plan.The ReviewTeamalsofoundthat:

   "Theexisting (sic, thepianbasinfact expired) National Forest Planwas preparedby
   a committee set up by the Minister in 1996, rather than by the PNG ForestAuthority
   as required under the A d It would appear that the main reason for taking
   independent action was the Minister's inability to issue new timber permits in the
   absence of a National Forest Plan (s54). The Plan has not been updated, and
   projectsnot listedintha Plan have been recommendedto the PNG ForestAuthority
   Board for development. This review of "in progress" forestry projects identified a
   numberofthese indicatingthat s54 iseffectively beingignored."

                       AIAMBAK-KIUNGA ROADTIMBER AUTHORITY

AUDIT CONCLUSIONS:

RESOURCEISSUES:

This project is in breach of the National Forest Policy 1991and the current provisions of
the Forestry Act 1991 (as amended) in that the logging taking place either side of the
road corridor is not regulatedthrough either a proper Forest ManagementAgreement or
a proper Timber Authority. The project cannot be consideredto be a forestry project in
any proper forest management sense. The project is also in breach of the Act in that
loggingappears to have moved intothe East Awin Forest ManagementAgreement area
withoutdue allocation processbeingobserved.

LEGAL ISSUES:

At no time since the purported issuance of the Timber Authority in April 1994 has any
aspect of due process been observed. This has arisen from matters beyond the direct
controlof the PNGFA.

LANDOWNER ISSUES:

There has been no landowner mobilisation for this project. The holder of the Timber
Authority claims to be a landowner company but is a foreign company (50% foreign
owned). The logging contractor Concord Pacific Ltd continues to pay less royalty than
K10 per cubic meter despite a letter from PNGFA directing the company to pay the

                                                                                         -
Review of "In Process" ForestryProjects
IndividualProject Report3I -Aiambak-Kiunga Road TA

legally mandated rate of K10 per m3 from the beginning of logging. Landowners
complainof harassment(includingfrom `Police')and threats with firearms.

RECOMMENDATIONS(INCLUDINGCORRECTIVEMEASURESIF REQUIRED):

   That Court action should be taken forthwith to review the Orders permitting the
    loggingoperationssince November 1999.

   That any application to extend the scope and term of this Timber Authority be
    soundly rejected.

    That any further application for road line clearing should be properiy considered
    underthe ForestryAct 1991in lightof the Amendments madeto the Act in 2000 and
    certifiedin January 2001.



Note: The individual project reports summarise the findings of the Review Team
 regardingmaterialcomplianceissues, and present project specific recommendationsfor
the consideration of the Interagency Forestry Review Committee. Separate reports
 produced at the end of the review process set out in more detail the audit procedures
 applied, and comments and recommendations regarding existing policies, legal
 requirementsand projectdevelopment processes.

 A. FORESTRY AND PLANNINGASPECTS

 The clearing of forested landfor roadingdevelopmentdoes not requirethe applicationof
 the same sectoral planning and control measures as are required for long term
 sustainable forestry projects under the National Forest Policy 1991, the Forestry Act
 1999 and the National Forestry Guidelines 1993. The key issues for road line clearing
 Timber Authoritiesare:

     To ensure that the proposed roading development is consistent with the National or
     Provincialtransportplans;that the funds for roadconstructionare availableand have
     been committed; and that responsibilityfor road maintenance is clearly defined and
     accepted;

     To ensurethat clearingthe road line of forest is undertakenin a progressivemanner
     in accordancewith the road constructionprogramme,thus protectingas much of the
     forest as is possibleagainst the potential non-performanceof the roading contractor;
     and

     That the road line clearing and road Construction is undertakenin an environmentally
     responsiblemanner.

 In this case the Timber Authority was allegedly issued in April 1994 before detailed
 procedures and requirements for Timber Authorities were developed. These
 requirementswere developedduring 1995,and recognisedthat the timber revenuefrom

 Review of "InProcess"forestry Projects
 individual Project Report 3I -Aiambak-Kiunga Road TA                               Page 1

a road line clearing operation would not meet the cost of road construction unless
logging of a much wider corridor was allowed. A maximum road line clearance of 40 m
was imposed and supported by a National Executive Council decision (113/96). To be
compliantwith the Act, any logging outside of this 40 m road line must be under either a
ForestManagementAgreement,or a Timber HarvestingTimberAuthority.

The Timber Authority procedures and requirements developed by the PNGFA also
required observance of environmentalstandardswhich for roadingconstructionwere set
out in the PNG LoggingCode of practice 1996.

The Aiambak-KiungaRoadTA does not meetthe current requirementsof the Act in two
key aspects:

    It allows logging outside of the 40 m wide road line without a Forest Management
    Agreement or a Timber HarvestingTimber Authority. The original Timber Authority
    document provides that "harvesting [may take place on] both sides of the proposed
    road alignment". The Logging and Marketing Agreement between the TA holder
    (Paiso Company Pty Ltd) andthe logger (Concord Pacific Pty Ltd) is more specific in
    that it providesfor logginga forest corridor up to 2 km in width, and the harvestingof
    4,200 m3 of logs for every kilometreof road constructed (or a total of 1.O million m3
    of logsfor the 246 km long road).

    Itdoes not requirefull observanceof the environmentalstandardsfor roadingset out
    in the PNG Logging Code of Practice 1996. The TA document provides for some
    rudimentaryobservanceof environmentalconcerns by requiringthe holder to "avoid"
    certainspecified impacts.Howeverthese obligationsare not clearly passed on to the
    logger in the Loggingand MarketingAgreement.

 The roading standard specified in the Logging and Marketing Agreement is a "dry
 weather 4x4 road. A road of this type in this wet part of PNG is unlikely to be usablefor
 any significant periods, or to last long without significant expenditure on road
 maintenance. It is difficult to see the demand which would justify this type of road.
 Generally this project is seen as a thinly disguised method employed by a logging
 companyto gain accessto logsfor export.

 RECOMMENDATIONSREGARDING FORESTRYASPECTS:

 1. That any application to extend the scope and term of this Timber Authority be
     soundly rejected.

 2. That any further applicationfor road line clearing be subjectedto the requirementof
     the Act as amended.


 B LEGALCOMPLIANCE
   ,

 SUMMARY OF LEGALCOMPLIANCE:

 Since Minister Neville purportedto approve TA No 24 in April 1994 no procedural step
 under the ForestryAct 1991 has been duly observed. In giving his approvalthe Minister


 Review of "In Process" Forestry Projects
 Individual Project Report 31 -Aiambak-Kiunga Road TA                               Page 2

exceededhis powers. Underthe Act Ministershave no proceduralrole to play in relation
to TimberAuthorities.

TA No 24 was signed by the Regional Forestry Inspector "as a delegate of the Board".
Under the Act it was the Provincial Forest Minister at that time who was empoweredto
grant a Timber Authority. Howeverthe Act prescribesa lengthy procedureto be followed
before such a grant can be made. That procedurewas completelyoverlooked in relation
to this project.

The subsequent decisions by Acting Minister Philemon and Minister Pok to grant
extensionsto TA No 24 are also not supported by provisions of the Act. The Act states
that Timber Authorities are to bevalidfor the stated period and makes no mentionof any
power to extend them. It seems that a new application must be made when a Timber
Authority expires. In any event there is no provision made at all for any power to be
exercisedbythe NationalMinisterfor Forests.

Logging operationsare proceedingon the basis of a far-reaching Court Order made on
26 November 1999.This Order permitsthe operations to continue under TA No 24 and
restrainsthe PNGFAfrom seizing logs or delaying export permits.

The current Managing Director and PNGFA staff have demonstrated a clear
determinationto see that some controlsare exercisedover this remarkableproject.Their
attempts to apply the provisions of the Act have been thwarted at every stage. The
 serious ramifications of the project affecting the rights of landowners and the good
 governanceof this country cannot be attributedto the current staff of the PNGFA in any
 way.

 NEC Submission 73/2000 (9 May 2000) seeks, inter alia, the approval of the NECfor a
 15 year extension to TA No 24. The Submission suggests that there are no legislative
 implications.This is incorrect and NEC should be aware that the Submission effectively
 invites Cabinet to breach the statutory procedures that are prescribed in the Forestry
 Act.

 RECOMMENDATIONSREGARDING LEGALASPECTS:

 1. That the court proceedingsmust be broughtto a conclusion.The substantive issues
     must be finally determined. It should be noted that the Attorney General has
     previouslyacted in a private capacityfor the logging operator (ConcordePacific Ltd)
     in relationto these proceedings.

                                    ExecutiveSummary

The Landownerand Community NaturalResourceSpecialistshaveexaminedallthe
projects on the list and have consulted with communities in a few of the project
areas. During the consultationwith communities there was occasion to review the
impact of priorloggingactivitiesinthe projectareaor inadjacentareas. Thiswas the
case in Rottock Bay, East Awin, Aiambak Road. One of the consultants has
previouslyconducteddetailed audiis of lartdownercompany perSormaneein several
existing projects,(See Samol and Romaso, 1997 and Romaso 1998). In addition to
these sources the Review Team received representationsfrom several NGOs with
detailedfeedback from land groups in many projects.

The reportbegins with an analysis of the Terms of Referenceto establish the
frameworkfor analysis, beginningwith an analysis of "consultation"andthen
developingthe implicationsof signing an FMA bothfor landowners andthe PNGFA.
Then follows an analysis of the impliedpolicies enfolded inthe ForestlyAct and
PNGFA administrativepracticesin relationto forest management for longterm
development.

The conclusion is inescapablethat the present FMA is flawed inthat there is no
partnershipbetween PNGFA andthe resourceowners. Indeedthe processexplicitly
excludes resourceowners from participatingin any agreements subsequentto the
FMA being signed. They enjoy the rightto be consulted only. Inthe opinion of the
ReviewTeam LandownerSpecialistswhere there is no explicit partnershipno sound
basis is provideduponwhichto managethe landownersforest for the longterm.

Specificallythe LandownerSpecialistsconcludedthat:

1      Landownersare in noway capable of makingan informeddecision regarding
       the 50 yeartransfer of their forest rightsto the Papua New Guinea National
       ForestAuthority. All FMAs signedto date as seen by the ReviewTeam are
       deficient from the point of informedconsent,
       s         given the very poor level or awareness regardingthe "project" and
                 purported"development";
                 given that there is no actualarea planfor developmentof the FMAfor
       *        them to understandand agreeto anyway;
                 given the confusionandthe almost completelack of empowerment
                 accomplishedbythe presentlandgroup incorporationprocess as
                 seen inthe forest sector; and
                 given that correct protocolsfor consultationand signing of the
                 documentation have neverbeen implemented.

2      There is noway that PNGFAcan accomplishwhat it purportsto via the FMA ,
                 giventhat development boils down to merelystagedharvestingof the
                 forest over 35/40 years accordingto five year rollingforest working
                 plans for round log export:



Review of "In Process" Forestry Projects -Observations and Recommendations
Avoendix 6                                                                         Pape 1

       e        giventhatthere is no actualdevelopmentplan for the forest and the
                forest dwellers, not a five year plan let alone a 50 year plan;
       e        giventhat there is no actualbusinessplanto involve landowners;and
       0        given that there is no actualsocioeconomicinfrastructureplan other
                than awatered down landownerwish list with little or no Local-level
                Govemmentbuyin.
       e        Giventhatthere is no partnershipbetween landownersand PNGFA.

Recommendations

Thus the ReviewTeam LandownerSpecialists concludethatthe present protocols
for mobilizingthe forest underthe FMA s needs to be seriously reconsidered and
modifiedto makethe forest owners part and parcel of the process and part and
parcel of the development. Specifically,

       Full development planningof the FMAwith full involvementof landowners
       needs to be conductedpriorto any agreementsbeingsigned. A good start
       would be to followthe requirementsof the 1993 NationalForestry
       DevelopmentGuidelinesandthe requirementsofthe EnvironmentAct. These
       plans must be fleshed out to includebusiness plansthat fully involve the
       forest ownersfrom day one as part of a full awareness programme.

       Landownersmust be mobilizedexpertlyusingthe provisions of the Land
       Groups IncorporationAct which itself must be expertly managed by the
       govemment instrumentalityresponsible. This mobilizationwill include
       ongoing extensionservicesinthe fieldwhile project planningand preparation
       is being carriedout.

       Given that there is no expert capability in DLPPto implement the Land
       Groups IncorporationAct, serious considerationneedsto be given to
       establish an arm of govemmentthat can fulfill this role. See separate
       recommendation.

       The FMA must be modifiedto follow morethe modelof the MOUs being
       signed betweenthe project area people andthe State in the non-renewable
       extractive resourceprojects.The forest owners must become full partners in
       the planned development, not bystanders.

The LandownerSpecialistsproposea developmentmethodologythat will embrace
the aims of the ForestryAct in a way that will addressthe requirementsof longterm
forestry and landowner equityand development. Of central importanceisthe
proposalto form a Forest ManagementArea Corporationto take on responsibilityto
work with all stakeholdersincludingPNGFAto plan and implement development in
the Forest ManagementArea. It is proposedto expandthe present FMAagreement
to embracethe requirementsof a comprehensivedevelopmentMOU.

Finally,a comprehensivereformof the implementationof the Land Groups
IncorporationAct is criticalto landowner developmentin a Forest ManagementArea
as anywhere else. Specific recommendationsare made for the empowermentof
governmentto implementthis importantlegislation, not only for forestry but for all
forms of resource owner development.

Sustainable harvestingof rainforesttrees under a selectivelogging system is
recognised as ecologicallyand economically preferableto clear fell timber
extractionwhen the whole rangeof values of the forest is considered(Leslie
1987; Perry & Maghembe1989). Indeed,the Papua New Guineagovernment
believes selective logging of rainforestsand a policy of sustained yield            .)
managementshould provide longterm benefitsto the communitieswho own the
forests. With the implementationof the LoggingCode of Practice(1996) the PNG
government consolidated and simplifiedthe conditionsfor timber extractionfrom
a previously largerangeof documentsconcernedwith logging. The code
prescribes best management practicesfor selection loggingand aims to reduce
the adverse impacts of loggingon the forests andthe communities living in them'.

The Wawoi GuaviTimber Company (WGTC)also recognisesthat sustainedyield
managementis beneficialto the longterm economic viability of their Kamusi
based project(see WGTC 1988)and have prescribedthemselves to the
selective harvestingmethodof timber extraction.This company should be guided
by the PNG LoggingCode of Practiceto ensurethat they have a sustainedyield,           .
while independent monitoringof loggingactivities isthe responsibilityof the PNG
ForestAuthority (Nadarajah 1994).However, customarylandownersof the
Wawoi Guavitimber area believethat WGTC and the ForestryAuthority have not
ensured that negligent practicesdo notoccur. This report presentsan
assessmentof these allegations and the environmentalimpacts of logging in
Wawoi Guavi Block 3.




 Block3 of the Wawoi Guavitimber area covers 206 310 hectaresof mixed
 lowland rainforestsome 140 kilometresnortheastof Daru, inthe Western
 Provinceof Papua New Guinea. The forest is generallytall (30-35 metres high),
 structurally complex and floristically rich (Paijmans 1976).It is the customaryland       u
 of 10clanswho live in surroundingvillages and it contains anumber of gardens,.
 sacred sites and forest resourcesthat are utilised by landowners.




[EL4of Wawoi GuaviBlock 3

                                                                                   4




An assessmentof the WGTC loggingoperationsin $lock 3 was undertakenfrom
the 23rd - 26thof February 1999. Loggingand roadconstruction practiceswere
examinedto determine compliancewith the PNG LoggingCode of Practice
(1996). Excludedproducefrom Schedule6 of the Wawoi GuaviTimber Rights
Purchase(seeAppendix I)       which had been affected by loggingoperationswere       -
also located.A further examinationof road maintenanceand decommissioningof
logging sites in Block 2 of the Wawoi GuaviTRP was used to predictthe long-
term environmentalconsequencesof loggingand road building.All sites
examined were locatedwith a MagellanColorTRAKGlobalPositioningSystem
(GPS)which is accurateto 25 metres.The slopesof tracks were measuredwith
a Suunto clinometer.


 Incidental loss

At the end of different snigtracks (alllessthan 2 monthsold) an estimateof the
total number of killedtrees was made in six plots rangingin size from 500 to
 1050m2.Any stem over 10 cm diameterat breast height (dbh)that had been
 logged, uprootedor pushedoverwas defined as havingbeen killed. Itwas not
 possibleto determineconsistentlywhether a tree had been lost to roadworks or
tree falls so all killedtrees were classifiedas `incidentalloss'.




 Conditionsfrom the Timber RightsPurchase(TRP), the agreed criteriaand key
 standards from the PNG LoggingCode of Practice are written in boldtext in this
 section. Violations of these conditionsand standards are in normaltext.


 Agreed criteria: logging on slopes greater than 30 degrees and extraction of trees
 with a diameter at breast height @BH) of less than 50 cm is prohibited.

      Trees had beenextracted and hauled up slopes measuredat 31 and 34
      degrees (07'14'12"S, 142'47'1 5"E).

      A number of undersizedtrees were found bothon sniggingtracks and in log
      landings. On one snig track there were 4 logs ranging in size from 28 to 43
      cm dbh and in a nearby log landing2 undersizelogswere found



I EL4ofWawoi GuaviBlock 3

                                                                                 5


       (0707'20"S, 142'49'22"E). A previous survey of four roadside log landings
       by Gebia (1998) found three undersize logs.


 Key StandardNumber 2: MinimumBuffer Zone Widths

 2a. Minimumbuffer zone for culturalsites, reserves, conservationandgarden areas
 i s 100 metres.

       A garden owned by John Tanaiye was removed during road construction
       (0709'36"S, 14251'58"E).All that presently remains is a small patch of
       bamboo to the west of the road.


 2c. Minimumbuffer zone for lakes, lagoons, swamps (surface water presentfor 6
 months of the year) is 100metres.

       A tree was felled directly into a swamp damaging four sago palms
       (0710'25"S, 14251'39"E).An example of the damage to one of the sago
       palms can be seen in the foreground of Figure 1. The felled tree was rooted
       only 25 metres from the swamp and the required 100-metre buffer zone
       was unmarked and therefore ignored. Logging activities had disturbed
       another sago swamp that was unable to be reached due to poor road
       conditions.


 2d. Minimum buffer zone for a Class 1permanentstream(riverbedis more than 5
 metres inwidth) is 50 metres.

       Trees were felled within 10 metres of a Class 1 permanent stream near a
       bridge crossing (0714'21"S, 142'46'46"E).


 2e. Minimumbuffer zone for a Class 2 permanentstream(riverbed i s between 3
 and 5 metresinwidth) is 10 metres.

       Figure 2 illustrates one of many examples (0708'38"S, 142'51'45"E;
       0707'37"S, 142'49'21"E; 07'07'24"S, 14251'01"E; 0709'47"S,
        142'48'48"E) of clearing and logging into Class 2 permanent streams while
       the flow of one stream (Figure 3) was completely restricted by debris
       (0710'10"S, 142'48'42"E).




[EIA of Wawoi GuaviBlock 3

                                                                                  6




 2i. Markinginthe field (all bufferzones are to be markedbeforeapprovalto log a
 set-up is given).

      There was no evidence of bufferzones being marked in the field. Chainsaw
      and bulldozeroperators also suggested that buffer zones were not being
      marked.


 Key StandardNo. 4: N o more than 3 log landingsper set-up, maximumsize 2500 sq.
 metres each.

      Two examples of log landings larger than the maximum allowable size were
      found. One was 3300 m2 (07'08'35"S, 14251'15"E)and the other 2835 m2
      (07'1 0'16"S, 142'47'33"E). Furthermore,within a kilometer from the second
      oversize log landing there was 8 other log landings.


 Key StandardNo. 7: Use of compactionequipment (rollers) duringroad
 construction.

      The Wawoi Guavi Timber Company'sroller has not operated in over 6
      years and presently lies abandoned and overgrown with vegetation just off
       road 1.Therefore, tractor bulldozers and graters have constructed all of the
       roads in Block 3.


 Key StandardNo. 9: Maximumcleared forest roadwidth is 40 metres.

      The width of feeder roads and main road was greater than 40 metres at
       many locations. Two locations were measured to demonstrate this. At one
       point (07'09'28"S, 14252'00"E)on recently constructed feeder roads the
      width was 53 metres and at another point (07'10'23"S, 142'50'28"E) the
       road width was 61 metres. Furthermore,following fires lit by WGTC to clear
       roadside vegetation in 1997the width of the main road increased to more
      than 100 metres in many areas (see Figure4).


 KeyStandardNo. 10: N o soil in streams fromroadconstruction or skid tracks.

      There were several examples (07'09'28"S, 142'52'00"E; 0707'24"S,
       142'51 '01"E; 07'09'47"S, 142'48'48"E; 07'1 0'1O"S, 142'48'42"E) of soil
       being pushed into the streams during road construction.

 Key Standard No. 12: Soil on log bridges.

      Soil was used as decking material on all bridges that were found in Block 3.
      The requirementto use rot resistant decking materialto ensure longevity of
      the bridge and to prevent soil enteringthe streamseemed to be universally
      absent. None of the bridges located used rot resistant material between the
      log base and soil fill (07'08'38"S, 142'51'45"E; 07'07'37"S, 142'49'21"E;
      07'07'24"S, 142'51 '01"E; 07'09'47"S, 142'48'48"E; 07'14'21 "S,
      142'46'46"E; 07'1 0'1O"S, 142'48'42"E).

      A lo cluster consisting of 140 logs underthe road (07'1 1'46"S,
       142 56'30"E) was found in Block 3 (see Figure 5).

      Trees were often felled into the forest ratherthan towards the road or
       skidding tracks. At a pointwere the roadwidth was 61 metres (07'1 0'23'3,

       when decommissioning roads. Road access to these abandoned bridges in

       Dawabi River (a Class 1 stream) in Block 2 was found. Over 50 individual
       logs were blockingthe river at this point becausethe bridge had collapsed
       after the road had been decommissioned (Figure7).

      The compacted soil in one old log landing (07'1 1'12"S, 142'52'42"E) was

       Several metresof metalwinch cable from a tractor bulldozerand an old oil

 The company mustensurethat camp personnelare aware that they are not able to
 garden, hunt, fish or erect buildingsfor personaluse on land, river or sea.

       Several of the WGTC employees were seen fishing in a river near the

 Incidental losses

 The average number of trees (>lOcm dbh) that were lost during timber extraction
 was higher in the Wawoi Guavi logging operations than other studies on
 selection logging (see Table I). incident loss during logging operations at
                                    The
 Wawoi Guavi were more that 6 times that of the Australian study.


 Table 1. Data from various studies conducted at selective logging sites for comparison
 withdata from the Wawoi Guavi logging area.


  Place                          Average number of trees
                                 (>lo                        Study
                                      cmdbh) lost per
                                 tree extracted

  Amazonia, Brazil                           4.9             Uhl&Viena (1989)
  Kalimaiitan, Indonesia                     5.7             Abdulhadi et al. (1981)

  Wawoi Guavi, PNG                          16.7             This study (1999)




 Damage from roads and log landings

 It was observed that the WGTC had ignored many of the key standards from the
 PNG Logging Code of Practicewhen constructing,maintainingand
 decommissioning roads and log landings. Newly constructed roads in Block 3
 were often wider than 40 metres,while the width of some of the older roads in
 Blocks 2 and 3 were more than 3 times the allowable limit (ie. 125 metres wide at
 07'1 0'25"S, 142'51 '39"E). During road and log landing construction, trees were
 frequently pushed into the forest ratherthan felled towards roads or clearings.
 Furthermore, when roads and log landingswere decommissionedthe soil was
 not ripped and remained compacted. At other logging sites in PNG Lamb (1990)
 reports that forest regrowth was poor and stunted unless soil was deeply ripped
 by bulldozers at the completion of logging.The absence of active
 decommissioning practices will retard forest regeneration,while poor
 construction and maintenance practices have increasedthe incidental tree loss
 and disturbed large sections of the forest canopy.

1EW of Wawoi GuaviBlock 3                                                                 1

                                                                                   10




 Fire damage

 Roadside fires spread more than 100 metres into the forest and killed many trees
 during the 1997 droughts. Rainforesttrees generally have a low tolerance to fire
 and the long-term regenerationof rainforests depends on the exclusion of wildfire
 (Lamb 1990). Fire preventionis one of the key strategies in the WGTC
 environmental plan (1988) yet the fires, which burnt many hectares of forest in
 Wawoi Guavi TRP, were lit by the company to keepthe roadside vegetation
 down. By burning roadside vegetation during a drought the WGTC has not
 ensured that its environmental strategies were followed and has damaged the
 regeneration capacity of the forest.


 Soil erosion

 Soil erosion affects the water quality of streams by increasing nutrient levels and
 turbidity. By logging into streams, not using a rollerto compact road surfaces nor
 using rot resistant material in bridge construction, the WGTC has allowed
 excessive quantities of soil into many of the streams. Long-term effects are hard
 to predict, but in the short term the flora and fauna in many streams will be
 negatively affected, as they are generally adapted to low levels of nutrients and
 low turbidity.


 Logging damage - comparisons with other studies

 The problems associated with inventorywork in rainforest logging sites are
 rarely, if ever, properly addressed. In gathering the data in Table Ithere
 appeared to be an array of methods and little analysis of sampling biases.
 Nevertheless the data are probably robust enoughto accept the observation of
 relatively high incidental losses at the Wawoi Guavi timber area in comparison
 with the other studies. This appears to be the result of poor management
 practices rather than any significant difference between forests. Particularly, by
 not logging towards gaps, road or tracks, and bulldozerscircling logged trees,
 incidental losses would have increased.The only study that had similar levels of
 incidental losses was conducted in 1958 (see Table 1)when logging guidelines
 were not carefully regulated.The end result of the present management
 practices in Wawoi Guavi Block 3 is that fewer potentialtimber trees are kept to
 ensure sustainable long term harvests in this forest.




1EIA of Wawoi GuaviBlock 3

                                                                                   11




Damage to excludedproduce

Sago is a very important building material and the staple food for many of the
landowners in this region. Paijmans (1976) suggests that harvesting sago for
food and building causes little damage to the environment, as only the stems
about to flower are removed.As this species takes up to 15 years to mature, the
loss of many plants due to poor logging practices representquite a loss to the
landowners. If the WGTC had followed the loggingguidelines, there should have
been 100-metre buffer around each swamp and the timber trees should have
been felled in the opposite direction. Furthermore,as sago is listed in the
excluded produce section of the TRP, the WGTC has been particularly negligent
in damaging these palms.

Breadfruit, Artocarpus altilis, is an extremely importantfruit tree and wild plants
are often individually owned and tended (Paijmans 1976). Inthe rainforest the
large and unique leaves of breadfruittree make it difficult for it to be mistaken as
a different species. Therefore, although only one breadfruittree was seen cut
down, it represents another significant act of negligenceon the part of the
WGTC.


Effects on wildlife

While selective logging directly harms some wildlife, others are endangered by
the hunting that follows. Particularly, populationsof the larger birds such as
cassowaries (Casuarius spp.) and hornbills (Aceros spp.) are reduced with the
increased use of logging roads and shotguns (Lamb 1990). Furthermore, canopy
and understorey birds are known to decrease in numberswithin a disturbed
habitat (Driscoll 1985). Illegal hunting by WGTC employeeswould decrease the
number of birds and animals near the timber camps and may lead to the local
extinction of some species.


Effects on keystone species

Some plant and animal species are critical components in the maintenance and
regeneration of different ecosystems. Becauseof their important role to
ecosystem function they are known as keystone species (Begon et al. 1996).
Figs (Ficus spp.) are one of the most important keystone species in rainforests
as they provide a year-round fruit supply to many species of mammals and birds
(Lambert & Marshall 1991, Nason et al. 1998).The large numbers and range of
animals that visit figs disperse a variety of seeds around the base of each tree.
Figs therefore have a central role in the regenerationof disturbed rainforest sites
by allowing the reintroduction of many different plant species. Fortunately, the
branch architecture, variable growth form and poorwood quality of figs make it



EL4 of Wawoi Guavi Block 3                                                            ,I

                                                                                    12


 an undesirable timber species. Nevertheless,it was observedthat several figs
 were cut down in Block 3 but not taken for timber purposes.They may have been
 logged to make room for timber trees, but the loss of these trees will have
 damaged the regeneration capacity of the Wawoi Guavi forest.

 Cassowaries (Casuarius spp.) are also known to be a keystone species in Papua
 New Guinea rainforests as they ingest and disperse a large numberfruits
 (Lamothe et al. 1990). Furthermore, passagethrough the gut of the cassowary
 removes fruit flesh and enhancesthe germinationof seeds, while faecal deposits
 confer considerable ecological benefits by promoting seedling recruitment (Noble
 1991). The germination of several PNGtimber species are enhanced by
 cassowaries (Lamothe et al. 1990)and therefore the loss of cassowaries through
 hunting will ultimately harm the long-term sustainablity of the Wawoi Guavi timber
 project.


 Effects on landowners

 The loss of sago palms, breadfruittrees and gardensthrough negligent logging
 and road construction practices all represent a major nutritional loss to the
 landowners of Block 3. The activities of WGTC have also increased soil erosion
 and stream turbidity, which decreases fish and crayfish recruitment. Hunting of
 wildlife by WGTC employees has killed a number of animals and some of these
 animals, such as the cassowary, represent a significant economic loss to the
 landowners (Winslow 1977).

 Logs used to construct bridges and clusters, and logs discarded in the forest,
 were reportedly not paid for by WGTC. The WGTC informedthe landowners that            .
 the royalties received for logs were calculatedfrom the number of logs taken to
 Kamusi and not at stumpage. The loss of royaltiesfrom this practicewould be
 extremely large as one small bridge (07'1 1'12"S, 142'52'42"E) contains at least
 56 logs while the observed log cluster had 140 logs.



 CONCLUSIONS


 The WGTC has not complied with many of the key standards and criteria in the
 PNG Logging Code of Practice and has negligently killed plants that are listed as
 excluded produce in the TRP. Furthermore,they have allowed keystone species
 to be killed and roadside fires to spread ir;to the forest. The cumulative effect of
 such a number of poor management practices has compromisedthe long-term
 sustained yield of this forest.
