                        ANNEX M


        RESPONSEON CLAIMS RELATED TO ECONOMIC
VIABILITY OF LOGGING AND SUSTAINABLEFOREST MANAGEMENT


B    Industrial-scale Forestry is Not Economically or Ecologically Feasible in Papua New
     Guinea

         a    "hut, most importantlv,,for the purposes qf'this submission: un industrv whose
             sales revenue is less than its out-goings, cm industry that is uneconomic.
              "The uneconomic nuture of'the logging industry has been apparentjbr some time.
              Loggingcould oniv hejustified in very nurrow profit-and-loss terms.from the
             point of view of'an export loyger. In terms qf'real economy9cxternaiities were
              never taken into uccount. However. now prices are low, the industry is
              uneconomic. even in u narrow profit-and-loss sense. Goodfaith requires a
             government to look ut the rcui,.fill1or mre economic impact upon its econom-vof
             an activity.  ' *                                         [Request. Section 2. p. 61

              "Thepolicy mandate in OP 4 36 of'sustainable management of naturalforests is
             problematic in Pcipiru iVew Guinea '.s mixed-species forests. The evidencepoints to
              industrial scale sustainable forestry not being either economicuilv or ecologicullv
             jeasible. Reasons given in the Request are that the cost of restoring the forest to
                        "
              its original condition is very high; selective logging on an industrial scale tends to
              kill off high value. slow-growing hardwoods; logging companies do not observe
             the Logging Code o f Conduct and often manage to clearcut their concessions; and
              mortality o f 53 to 79 percent o f the trees left standing after selective logging has
              been reported in Papua New Guinea.                       [Request, Subsections 5.14 -
             3.241

     Response

     The Ecologicaland Economic Feasibilityof Sustainable Forest Managementin
     Tropical Moist Forest

     1 .     The Requesters claim (see page 6) that the logging industry is uneconomic. when
     externalities are taken into account. and that therefore its continuation or expansion is
     irrational. Inparagraph 5.16, the Request states that the available evidence points to
     sustainable forestry being neither economically nor ecologically feasible and asserts that
     "there are no examples in the world where industrial logging in a mixed tropical forest
     has shown to be ecologically or commercially sustainable". Earlier in paragraph 5.14. the
     Request refers to a paper by Bowies et ui (reproduced in Annex 16) which argues that
     sustainable forest management is not an effective tool for tropical forest conservation and
     funds should be directed to protected weas rather than to supporting sustainable forest
     management.

     2.      Some o f the broader issues relating to sustainability and viability are addressed
     below this paragraph. At this point. it can be said that the economic viability o f the
     logging sector cannot be judged on the basis o f its performance under current procedures
     and the revenue formula which appiies. It is in the nature o f the logging sector. as with
     many commodities. that the market is volatile. and it is not possible to assert on the basis
     of a relatively few number o f years whether the industry is viable in the longer term or

D    not. This is a decision that individual investors make. and the appropriate role o f the State
     is to ensure that it collects reasonable economic rent from forest operations. that
     landowners also receive an equitable share. and that rules governing sustainability and
     environmental protection are applied.

     3 .       Inno country -developed or developing. tropical or temperate - is there a forest
     that has been subject to several industrial scale cutting cycles accompanied by rigorous
     scientific monitoring to demonstrate that forest structure and species compositions have
     been maintained oi'er this period. The empirical research to undertake such an exercise
     would require hundreds o f years and the ecological concepts that underpin such issues
     have only emerged as matters for public policy and resource management practices over
     the last several decades.' Nor is there m y direct empirical evidence o f the natural
     changes in species composition and structure that would occur in these dynamic forest
     ecosystems in the complete absence of human intervention over similar time scales.

     4.        On the other hand. there is considerable empirical experience with the
     conservation and management o f all forest types for timber and other purposes. Over the
     last decade. this empirical knowledge of best practice has been codified and regularly
     updated in various principles. criteria and indicators of international and inter-
     governmental certification systems. In tropical forests. there is a long history o f deliberate
     silviculture going back many centuries and more than 200 years o f deliberate scientific
     timber management. The recent work by Dawkins and Philip (1998)demonstrates that
1    sustainable management o f tropical moist forests is not only feasible but that key
     operational techniques have been known for many decades and are being refined and
     improved in response to emerging researchand accumulating management experience.
     Failures o f implementation are due more to institutional and capacity issues than lack o f
     technical knowledge per se. Importantly. these are factors which can be addressed
     through development assistance and other measuressuch as the recently approved FCP.

     5.        The economic concern raised in this claim has already been addressed in part in
     the response to Claim #3 regarding the forestry revenue regime (seeparas.XX). The
     economic feasibility o f sustainable forest management in tropical forest has been the
     subject o f recent review (Pearce cf ai. 1999). In narrow financial terms based on timber
     outputs alone. sustainable forest management of tropical forest is capable of earning
     returns inexcess o f modest discount rates o f 5 percent and in some cases 10 percent. The
     fact that non-sustainable timber operations can earn more than this in the short term is not
     surprising. Howei,er, more comprehensive economic anaijxis of all environmental \,dues
     increases the relative attractiveness o f sustainable forest management as against the non-
     sustainable alternatives.

     6.        As key economic benefits are often public goods that may not immediately
     generate cash income for particular stakeholders. this may create difficulties in terms o f
     promoting consenlation and sustainable management alternatives to conventional timber
     harvesting. But Lvhile the prospects for sustainable forest management are low inthe

     The Questionof Appropriate Cutting Cycles

     7.       InParagraph 5.15 of the Request. it is claimed that "The PiVGForest Authorirv
     bases ~111its,forest management on a 3.f-vear.f~restrycycle or rotationperiod. There is
     no scicntlfic evidence that mixed species tropical rciinfi7rest.s will regenerate within u
     period of35 >*earsclfrer (heyhave heen logged. The esidcnce is to the contrary. High
     value hardwood species invnriably take longcr than 33 )rurs to regenerate. '*

     8.       It is true that the rotation length- defined as the time for a tropical hardwood (or
     any tree) to grow from germination to maturity - is usually much more than 35 years.
     However. in the context of tropical forest management. a rotation length is not
     synonymous with a "forestry cycle" or. as it is more commonly known. a cutting or
     harvesting cycle.

     9.       For clear-felling harvesting systems. the cutting cycle and the rotation length are
     synonymous. However, for selection logging systems (see further discussion below), the
1    harvesting practice involves only partial cutting with harvesting restricted to trees above a
     defined size (for example 50 c m diameter). The smaller trees are allowed to grow until
     the next harvest. Inventory plots gather information on both the nature and condition of
     residual stands and the growth rates they achieve after logging. Inthe equatorial climate
     and relatively young geological sub-stratums in much o f Papua New Guinea. it is likely
     that growth rates following lorrging would be about 0.40 m3/ha /year for logged stands
     left to benign neglect to 2.1 m':ha/yr         for stands with modest post harvesting treatment.'          '
     (Vigus. 19953. b).

     10.      Thus the suggestion o f a 35 year cutting cycle is a scientifically defensible interim
     measure while more detailed information is gathered. In this regard. it should be noted
     that more than 100 detailed growth and yield plots were established by the Papua New
     Guinea Forest Research Institute with support from the International Tropical Timber
     Organization and Australian Council for International and Agricultural Research in the
     period 1993-1998. Under the Sustainable Forest Management Component o f FCP (see
     Annex D). the data from these plots will be used for modeling to determine more specific
     cutting cycles for various forest types and geographic provinces. Technical assistance
     will be usedto analyze the plot data. undertake modeling and train Forests Authority
     staff. Technical assistance in this area focused on the linkage to management decision-
     making systems is also expected from a parallel AusAID project.

B    The Nature of Selection Logging

     11.     Inparagraph 5.17. the Request argues that "thesustainable management of'
     naturalforests is problematic in Papua New Guinea, in that selective logging, on an
     industrial scale. tends to kill-oflthe high value slow-growing hardwoods *'and that
     "selective logging destroys between 53 und 79% of'the high value trees. Elsewhere in
                                                                                 "
     paragraph 5.19. the Request states that "The term "selective logging" is a euphemismfor
     "high grading"....rhe logger removes the high-valued hardwood species which are
     generullv slower jyowing. The @ct of'this is to give living space to,fnster growing,    btlf
     commerciallv less vciluuble .softwoodspecies. The ecological hulance of 'the,forest is
     disrupted and a systematic degradation takes pluce into secondarv growth.

     12.     As noted above. the fundamental distinction in forest harvesting systems is
     between complete removal systems (clear-felling systems) and partial harvesting systems
     (selection systems). Clear-felling systems. by definition. remove all trees and. inthe
     context o f tropical forest ecosystems. will be replaced by non-forest covers or extensive
     areas o f degraded secondary forest regrowth. These forest ecosystems will require very
     long periods to redevelop high levels o f species and structural diversity and consequent
     timber and environmental \dues.

     13.     Intropical rainforest ecosystems. any selection logging system will be less
     intrusive and environmentally disruptive than a clear-felling system. The impact o f a
1    selection system will depend on the nature of the system being employed. Most
     experienced conservation and managements specialists. like the authors o f the Request,
     feel that in most timber rich tropical environments. a highgrading approach i s naive and
     will lead to significant degradation o f the forest. In Papua New Guinea. there is
     considerable evidence that both codes of practice and field compliance with these codes
     of practice can be significantly improved to enhance the environmental, economic and
     social sustainability o f forest management systems (see discussion on logging damage
     and its control below). Bringing about these improvements is a key focus o f the FCP
     under. in particular. component 3 (ii)- Forest Management and Planning.

     The Levels of LoggingDamage

     14.     Annex 16 of the Request presents logging damage data collected by Vigus and
     provides the support for the earlier statement that "selection logging ciestro-vshefween 53
     und 79percent of the highcr lrees (see paragraph 89). However, the Annex only
                                          "

     presents a small part of the tindings by Vigus. who demonstrates on several plots that
     have been established in logged over forest in Oro, East and West Britain and West Sepik
     Provinces of Papua New Guinea that:

         0   Logging practices used in Papua New Guinea do invariably cause damage and
             loss to seedlings. saplings and a significant number o f residual trees (50 to 80
             percent loss ifresiduals after 8 to 10 years);

         0   Regeneration is profuse following logging;

          0   Species regeneratewith location. but invariably undesirable species such as Mum
              spp and Macurango spp were prevalent. as are other fast growing species of low
              to mediumvalue. while vines grow vigorously, often to the detriment of smaller
              commercial tree species:

          0   Many residual trees show little or no growth response 8 to 10 years after logging,
              andthose that do are invariably those that had not beendamagedby logging;

          0   Benign neglect of the stand 10 years after logging results inan impoverished
              stand from a commercial species perspective that is likely to result in a much
              reducedvalue stand after 35 to 40 years. Vigus reports treated stands with nearly
              four times the number of commercial trees per ha as neglectedstands:

          0   Removal of weed species. thinning of non-commercial species and the cutting o f
              vines for two years after logging results insubstantial growth and domination o f
              the stand by medium value commercial species ,such as Octomeles sumatrana.
             Anthocephalris chinenisis. and Endospernum sp. These are present largely as a
              result of recruitment from seedling regeneration: and

          0   Relatively low cost stand treatments have the potential to result in significant
              improvements in the value of the stand for the next cutting cycle. Vigus calculates
              a financial rate of return of 12 percent using a weighted log price of K130/m' and
              a production of 75m'/ha, compared with a first harvest averageof 20-25 m3/ha
              and 15m3/hafor loggedstands left to benign neglect.
B      15.    Thus, rather than demonstratingthe inevitability of excessive damage and reduced
     quality and economic value of forests being logged, the work of Vigus demonstratesthe
     potential of harvesting control and stand management to overcome this. Llrgusestimates
     that effective post harvesttreatments would cost as little as 72Wha and that improved
     harvesting would reduce both the level of logging damage and the cost of post-harvesting
     treatments.

       16.    Inthis regard. the Center for Intemational Forest Research(CIFOR) has. over the
     last several years. given high priority to research that has quantified the environmental
     effects of reduced impact logging (RIL). As part o f its broader research programs on
     sustainable forest management, CIFOR has worked with a range of partners to undertake
     RIL studies in Brazil. Bolivia. Cameroon, Guyana. Indonesia. Malaysia. Tanzania and
     Zambia (CIFOR. 1998).'

       17.    This researchhas demonstratedthat environmental damage can be minimized
     through the use of site-sensitive harvestingtechniques. Among the findings, it has found
     that RIL methods can reduce impacts on the soil from heavy logging machines by 25
     percent. and lead to a gain of as much as 50 percent inthe carbon storehousebenefits
      from the remaining vegetation. Insome RIL experiments in lowland tropical forests, the
     amount of damage to the soil and to advanced regeneration was reducedby about 50
     percent relative to conventional logging.

     Sustainable Forest ManagementComponent. PapuaNew Guinea Forestry and Conservation Project.

B    18.     InPapuaNew Guinea. as inmost countries. resource owners and those
     responsible for managing the resource face limited options: the possibilities for protecting
     large tracts o f forest from any major use whatever are limited. For the remainder. the
     choice comes down to whether that forest is used well. and sustainably, or whether it is
     allowed to degrade under destructive and unsustainable operations. While the task of
     gaining control over forest operations and implementinglow impact and conservation
     technologies should not be minimized. the FCP has the potential to make a material
     difference in outcomes in this regard. It will mobilize considerable resources (US$8.71
     million) to promote more sustainable forest management to reduce logging damage. and
     to promote effective post harvesting management to maintain the value o f forest
     resources for both traditional landholders and the nation as a whole. These resources \vi11
     be allocated under the FCP to improved forest operations. inspection and monitoring
     (Component 3.1 o f the Project - US $3.72 million); forest management planning
     (Component 3.2 ofthe Project - I!S$1.83     million); and post-harvest forest management
     and plantations (US$3.16 million). This will be supplemented by additional support of
     some US$4.68 million for environmental assessment and monitoring, including some
     US$0.83 million for environmental monitoring by local communities.

     19.     The FCP \vi11 also be able to contribute to solutions to the problem ofcontrolling
     logging damage and its impact on future forest values. Key aspects that the FCP will
     promote inthese areas will include:


1        e   Full involvement and commitment o f the landholders, with them being advised of
             the operational meaning o f sustainable forest management and the prerequisites
             for this;

         e   Promoting effective co-operation and communication between the NFS,
             landholders and forest industry companies:

             Tree felling and extraction carefully planned and controlled. including the
             marking and retention o f undamaged residuals: and

             Conscientiously undertaking treatments to promote the emergence and growth o f
             marketable species.
